```
01114| This sets up a timer that calls the function function with arguments args at time time. If repeat is a number (integer or floating point), the timer is scheduled to run again every repeat seconds after time. If repeat is nil, the timer runs only once.
01115| 
01116| time may specify an absolute or a relative time.
01117| 
01118| Absolute times may be specified using a string with a limited variety of formats, and are taken to be times today, even if already in the past. The recognized forms are ‘xxxx’, ‘x:xx’, or ‘xx:xx’ (military time), and ‘xxam’, ‘xxAM’, ‘xxpm’, ‘xxPM’, ‘xx:xxam’, ‘xx:xxAM’, ‘xx:xxpm’, or ‘xx:xxPM’. A period can be used instead of a colon to separate the hour and minute parts.
01119| 
01120| To specify a relative time as a string, use numbers followed by units. For example:
01121| 
01122| ‘1 min’
01123| denotes 1 minute from now.
01124| 
01125| ‘1 min 5 sec’
01126| denotes 65 seconds from now.
01127| 
01128| ‘1 min 2 sec 3 hour 4 day 5 week 6 fortnight 7 month 8 year’
01129| denotes exactly 103 months, 123 days, and 10862 seconds from now.
01130| 
01131| For relative time values, Emacs considers a month to be exactly thirty days, and a year to be exactly 365.25 days.
01132| 
01133| Not all convenient formats are strings. If time is a number (integer or floating point), that specifies a relative time measured in seconds. The result of encode-time can also be used to specify an absolute value for time.
01134| 
01135| In most cases, repeat has no effect on when first call takes place—time alone specifies that. There is one exception: if time is t, then the timer runs whenever the time is a multiple of repeat seconds after the epoch. This is useful for functions like display-time. For instance, the following will make function run at every “whole” minute (e.g., ‘11:03:00’, ‘11:04:00’, etc):
01136| 
01137| (run-at-time t 60 function)
01138| If Emacs didn’t get any CPU time when the timer would have run (for example if the system was busy running another process or if the computer was sleeping or in a suspended state), the timer will run as soon as Emacs resumes and is idle.
01139| 
01140| The function run-at-time returns a timer value that identifies the particular scheduled future action. You can use this value to call cancel-timer (see below).
01141| 
01142| Command: run-with-timer secs repeat function &rest args ¶
01143| This is exactly the same as run-at-time (so see that definition for an explanation of the parameters; secs is passed as time to that function), but is meant to be used when the delay is specified in seconds.
01144| 
01145| A repeating timer nominally ought to run every repeat seconds, but remember that any invocation of a timer can be late. Lateness of one repetition has no effect on the scheduled time of the next repetition. For instance, if Emacs is busy computing for long enough to cover three scheduled repetitions of the timer, and then starts to wait, it will immediately call the timer function three times in immediate succession (presuming no other timers trigger before or between them). If you want a timer to run again no less than n seconds after the last invocation, don’t use the repeat argument. Instead, the timer function should explicitly reschedule the timer.
01146| 
01147| User Option: timer-max-repeats ¶
01148| This variable’s value specifies the maximum number of times to repeat calling a timer function in a row, when many previously scheduled calls were unavoidably delayed.
01149| 
01150| Macro: with-timeout (seconds timeout-forms…) body… ¶
01151| Execute body, but give up after seconds seconds. If body finishes before the time is up, with-timeout returns the value of the last form in body. If, however, the execution of body is cut short by the timeout, then with-timeout executes all the timeout-forms and returns the value of the last of them.
01152| 
01153| This macro works by setting a timer to run after seconds seconds. If body finishes before that time, it cancels the timer. If the timer actually runs, it terminates execution of body, then executes timeout-forms.
01154| 
01155| Since timers can run within a Lisp program only when the program calls a primitive that can wait, with-timeout cannot stop executing body while it is in the midst of a computation—only when it calls one of those primitives. So use with-timeout only with a body that waits for input, not one that does a long computation.
01156| 
01157| The function y-or-n-p-with-timeout provides a simple way to use a timer to avoid waiting too long for an answer. See Yes-or-No Queries.
01158| 
01159| Function: cancel-timer timer ¶
01160| This cancels the requested action for timer, which should be a timer—usually, one previously returned by run-at-time or run-with-idle-timer. This cancels the effect of that call to one of these functions; the arrival of the specified time will not cause anything special to happen.
01161| 
01162| The list-timers command lists all the currently active timers. The command c (timer-list-cancel) will cancel the timer on the line under point. You can sort the list by column using the command S (tabulated-list-sort).
01163| 
01164| 
01165| *** Idle Timers
01166| 
01167| Here is how to set up a timer that runs when Emacs is idle for a certain length of time. Aside from how to set them up, idle timers work just like ordinary timers.
01168| 
01169| Command: run-with-idle-timer secs repeat function &rest args ¶
01170| Set up a timer which runs the next time Emacs is idle for secs seconds. The value of secs may be a number or a value of the type returned by current-idle-time.
01171| 
01172| If repeat is nil, the timer runs just once, the first time Emacs remains idle for a long enough time. More often repeat is non-nil, which means to run the timer each time Emacs remains idle for secs seconds.
01173| 
01174| The function run-with-idle-timer returns a timer value which you can use in calling cancel-timer (see Timers for Delayed Execution).
01175| 
01176| Emacs becomes idle when it starts waiting for user input (unless it waits for input with a timeout, see Reading One Event), and it remains idle until the user provides some input. If a timer is set for five seconds of idleness, it runs approximately five seconds after Emacs first becomes idle. Even if repeat is non-nil, this timer will not run again as long as Emacs remains idle, because the duration of idleness will continue to increase and will not go down to five seconds again.
01177| 
01178| Emacs can do various things while idle: garbage collect, autosave or handle data from a subprocess. But these interludes during idleness do not interfere with idle timers, because they do not reset the clock of idleness to zero. An idle timer set for 600 seconds will run when ten minutes have elapsed since the last user command was finished, even if subprocess output has been accepted thousands of times within those ten minutes, and even if there have been garbage collections and autosaves.
01179| 
01180| When the user supplies input, Emacs becomes non-idle while executing the input. Then it becomes idle again, and all the idle timers that are set up to repeat will subsequently run another time, one by one.
01181| 
01182| Do not write an idle timer function containing a loop which does a certain amount of processing each time around, and exits when (input-pending-p) is non-nil. This approach seems very natural but has two problems:
01183| 
01184| It blocks out all process output (since Emacs accepts process output only while waiting).
01185| It blocks out any idle timers that ought to run during that time.
01186| Similarly, do not write an idle timer function that sets up another idle timer (including the same idle timer) with secs argument less than or equal to the current idleness time. Such a timer will run almost immediately, and continue running again and again, instead of waiting for the next time Emacs becomes idle. The correct approach is to reschedule with an appropriate increment of the current value of the idleness time, as described below.
01187| 
01188| Function: current-idle-time ¶
01189| If Emacs is idle, this function returns the length of time Emacs has been idle, using the same format as current-time (see Time of Day).
01190| 
01191| When Emacs is not idle, current-idle-time returns nil. This is a convenient way to test whether Emacs is idle.
01192| 
01193| The main use of current-idle-time is when an idle timer function wants to “take a break” for a while. It can set up another idle timer to call the same function again, after a few seconds more idleness. Here’s an example:
01194| 
01195| (defvar my-resume-timer nil
01196|   "Timer for `my-timer-function' to reschedule itself, or nil.")
01197| 
01198| (defun my-timer-function ()
01199|   ;; If the user types a command while my-resume-timer
01200|   ;; is active, the next time this function is called from
01201|   ;; its main idle timer, deactivate my-resume-timer.
01202|   (when my-resume-timer
01203|     (cancel-timer my-resume-timer))
01204|   ...do the work for a while...
01205|   (when taking-a-break
01206|     (setq my-resume-timer
01207|           (run-with-idle-timer
01208|             ;; Compute an idle time break-length
01209|             ;; more than the current value.
01210|             (time-add (current-idle-time) break-length)
01211|             nil
01212|             'my-timer-function))))
01213| 
01214| *** Terminal Input
01215| 
01216| This section describes functions and variables for recording or manipulating terminal input. See Emacs Display, for related functions.
01217| 
01218| **** Input Modes
01219| 
01220| Function: set-input-mode interrupt flow meta &optional quit-char ¶
01221| This function sets the mode for reading keyboard input. If interrupt is non-nil, then Emacs uses input interrupts. If it is nil, then it uses CBREAK mode. The default setting is system-dependent. Some systems always use CBREAK mode regardless of what is specified.
01222| 
01223| When Emacs communicates directly with X, it ignores this argument and uses interrupts if that is the way it knows how to communicate.
01224| 
01225| If flow is non-nil, then Emacs uses XON/XOFF (C-q, C-s) flow control for output to the terminal. This has no effect except in CBREAK mode.
01226| 
01227| The argument meta controls support for input character codes above 127. If meta is t, Emacs converts characters with the 8th bit set into Meta characters, before it decodes them as needed (see Terminal I/O Encoding). If meta is nil, Emacs disregards the 8th bit; this is necessary when the terminal uses it as a parity bit. If meta is the symbol encoded, Emacs first decodes the characters using all the 8 bits of each byte, and then converts the decoded single-byte characters into Meta characters if they have their eighth bit set. Finally, if meta is neither t nor nil nor encoded, Emacs uses all 8 bits of input unchanged, both before and after decoding them. This is good for terminals that use 8-bit character sets and don’t encode the Meta modifier as the eighth bit.
01228| 
01229| If quit-char is non-nil, it specifies the character to use for quitting. Normally this character is C-g. See Quitting.
01230| 
01231| The current-input-mode function returns the input mode settings Emacs is currently using.
01232| 
01233| Function: current-input-mode ¶
01234| This function returns the current mode for reading keyboard input. It returns a list, corresponding to the arguments of set-input-mode, of the form (interrupt flow meta quit) in which:
01235| 
01236| interrupt
01237| is non-nil when Emacs is using interrupt-driven input. If nil, Emacs is using CBREAK mode.
01238| 
01239| flow
01240| is non-nil if Emacs uses XON/XOFF (C-q, C-s) flow control for output to the terminal. This value is meaningful only when interrupt is nil.
01241| 
01242| meta
01243| is t if Emacs treats the eighth bit of input characters as the Meta bit before decoding input; encoded if Emacs treats the eighth bit of the decoded single-byte characters as the Meta bit; nil if Emacs clears the eighth bit of every input character; any other value means Emacs uses all eight bits as the basic character code.
01244| 
01245| quit
01246| is the character Emacs currently uses for quitting, usually C-g.
01247| 
01248| 
01249| **** Recording Input
01250| 
01251| Function: recent-keys &optional include-cmds ¶
01252| This function returns a vector containing the last 300 input events from the keyboard or mouse. All input events are included, whether or not they were used as parts of key sequences. Thus, you always get the last 300 input events, not counting events generated by keyboard macros. (These are excluded because they are less interesting for debugging; it should be enough to see the events that invoked the macros.)
01253| 
01254| If include-cmds is non-nil, complete key sequences in the result vector are interleaved with pseudo-events of the form (nil . COMMAND), where COMMAND is the binding of the key sequence (see Command Loop Overview).
01255| 
01256| A call to clear-this-command-keys (see Information from the Command Loop) causes this function to return an empty vector immediately afterward.
01257| 
01258| Command: open-dribble-file filename ¶
01259| This function opens a dribble file named filename. When a dribble file is open, each input event from the keyboard or mouse (but not those from keyboard macros) is written in that file. A non-character event is expressed using its printed representation surrounded by ‘<…>’. Be aware that sensitive information (such as passwords) may end up recorded in the dribble file.
01260| 
01261| You close the dribble file by calling this function with an argument of nil.
01262| 
01263| 
01264| *** Terminal Output
01265| 
01266| The terminal output functions send output to a text terminal, or keep track of output sent to the terminal. The variable baud-rate tells you what Emacs thinks is the output speed of the terminal.
01267| 
01268| User Option: baud-rate ¶
01269| This variable’s value is the output speed of the terminal, as far as Emacs knows. Setting this variable does not change the speed of actual data transmission, but the value is used for calculations such as padding.
01270| 
01271| It also affects decisions about whether to scroll part of the screen or repaint on text terminals. See Forcing Redisplay, for the corresponding functionality on graphical terminals.
01272| 
01273| The value is measured in baud.
01274| 
01275| If you are running across a network, and different parts of the network work at different baud rates, the value returned by Emacs may be different from the value used by your local terminal. Some network protocols communicate the local terminal speed to the remote machine, so that Emacs and other programs can get the proper value, but others do not. If Emacs has the wrong value, it makes decisions that are less than optimal. To fix the problem, set baud-rate.
01276| 
01277| Function: send-string-to-terminal string &optional terminal ¶
01278| This function sends string to terminal without alteration. Control characters in string have terminal-dependent effects. (If you need to display non-ASCII text on the terminal, encode it using one of the functions described in Explicit Encoding and Decoding.) This function operates only on text terminals. terminal may be a terminal object, a frame, or nil for the selected frame’s terminal. In batch mode, string is sent to stdout when terminal is nil.
01279| 
01280| One use of this function is to define function keys on terminals that have downloadable function key definitions. For example, this is how (on certain terminals) to define function key 4 to move forward four characters (by transmitting the characters C-u C-f to the computer):
01281| 
01282| (send-string-to-terminal "\eF4\^U\^F")
01283|      ⇒ nil
01284| Command: open-termscript filename ¶
01285| This function is used to open a termscript file that will record all the characters sent by Emacs to the terminal. It returns nil. Termscript files are useful for investigating problems where Emacs garbles the screen, problems that are due to incorrect Termcap entries or to undesirable settings of terminal options more often than to actual Emacs bugs. Once you are certain which characters were actually output, you can determine reliably whether they correspond to the Termcap specifications in use.
01286| 
01287| (open-termscript "../junk/termscript")
01288|      ⇒ nil
01289| You close the termscript file by calling this function with an argument of nil.
01290| 
01291| See also open-dribble-file in Recording Input.
01292| 
01293| 
01294| *** Sound Output
01295| 
01296| To play sound using Emacs, use the function play-sound. Only certain systems are supported; if you call play-sound on a system which cannot really do the job, it gives an error.
01297| 
01298| The sound must be stored as a file in RIFF-WAVE format (‘.wav’) or Sun Audio format (‘.au’).
01299| 
01300| Function: play-sound sound ¶
01301| This function plays a specified sound. The argument, sound, has the form (sound properties...), where the properties consist of alternating keywords (particular symbols recognized specially) and values corresponding to them.
01302| 
01303| Here is a table of the keywords that are currently meaningful in sound, and their meanings:
01304| 
01305| :file file
01306| This specifies the file containing the sound to play. If the file name is not absolute, it is expanded against the directory data-directory.
01307| 
01308| :data data
01309| This specifies the sound to play without need to refer to a file. The value, data, should be a string containing the same bytes as a sound file. We recommend using a unibyte string.
01310| 
01311| :volume volume
01312| This specifies how loud to play the sound. It should be a number in the range of 0 to 1. The default is to use whatever volume has been specified before.
01313| 
01314| :device device
01315| This specifies the system device on which to play the sound, as a string. The default device is system-dependent.
01316| 
01317| Before actually playing the sound, play-sound calls the functions in the list play-sound-functions. Each function is called with one argument, sound.
01318| 
01319| Command: play-sound-file file &optional volume device ¶
01320| This function is an alternative interface to playing a sound file specifying an optional volume and device.
01321| 
01322| Variable: play-sound-functions ¶
01323| A list of functions to be called before playing a sound. Each function is called with one argument, a property list that describes the sound.
01324| 
01325| 
01326| *** Operating on X11 Keysyms
01327| 
01328| To define system-specific X11 keysyms, set the variable system-key-alist.
01329| 
01330| Variable: system-key-alist ¶
01331| This variable’s value should be an alist with one element for each system-specific keysym. Each element has the form (code . symbol), where code is the numeric keysym code (not including the vendor-specific bit, −2**28), and symbol is the name for the function key.
01332| 
01333| For example (168 . mute-acute) defines a system-specific key (used by HP X servers) whose numeric code is −2**28 + 168.
01334| 
01335| It is not crucial to exclude from the alist the keysyms of other X servers; those do no harm, as long as they don’t conflict with the ones used by the X server actually in use.
01336| 
01337| The variable is always local to the current terminal, and cannot be buffer-local. See Multiple Terminals.
01338| 
01339| You can specify which keysyms Emacs should use for the Control, Meta, Alt, Hyper, and Super modifiers by setting these variables:
01340| 
01341| Variable: x-ctrl-keysym ¶
01342| Variable: x-alt-keysym ¶
01343| Variable: x-meta-keysym ¶
01344| Variable: x-hyper-keysym ¶
01345| Variable: x-super-keysym ¶
01346| The name of the keysym that should stand for the Control modifier (respectively, for Alt, Meta, Hyper, and Super). For example, here is how to swap the Meta and Alt modifiers within Emacs:
01347| 
01348| (setq x-alt-keysym 'meta)
01349| (setq x-meta-keysym 'alt)
01350| 
01351| *** Batch Mode
01352| 
01353| The command-line option ‘-batch’ causes Emacs to run noninteractively. In this mode, Emacs does not read commands from the terminal, it does not alter the terminal modes, and it does not expect to be outputting to an erasable screen. The idea is that you specify Lisp programs to run; when they are finished, Emacs should exit. The way to specify the programs to run is with ‘-l file’, which loads the library named file, or ‘-f function’, which calls function with no arguments, or ‘--eval=form’.
01354| 
01355| Variable: noninteractive ¶
01356| This variable is non-nil when Emacs is running in batch mode.
01357| 
01358| If the specified Lisp program signals an unhandled error in batch mode, Emacs exits with a non-zero exit status after invoking the Lisp debugger which shows the Lisp backtrace (see Invoking the Debugger) on the standard error stream:
01359| 
01360| $ emacs -Q --batch --eval '(error "foo")'; echo $?
01361| 
01362| Error: error ("foo")
01363| mapbacktrace(#f(compiled-function (evald func args flags) #<bytecode -0x4f85c5
01364| 7c45e2f81>))
01365| debug-early-backtrace()
01366| debug-early(error (error "foo"))
01367| signal(error ("foo"))
01368| error("foo")
01369| eval((error "foo") t)
01370| command-line-1(("--eval" "(error \"foo\")"))
01371| command-line()
01372| normal-top-level()
01373| foo
01374| 255
01375| Any Lisp program output that would normally go to the echo area, either using message, or using prin1, etc., with t as the stream (see Output Streams), goes instead to Emacs’s standard descriptors when in batch mode: message writes to the standard error descriptor, while prin1 and other print functions write to the standard output. Similarly, input that would normally come from the minibuffer is read from the standard input descriptor. Thus, Emacs behaves much like a noninteractive application program. (The echo area output that Emacs itself normally generates, such as command echoing, is suppressed entirely.)
01376| 
01377| Non-ASCII text written to the standard output or error descriptors is by default encoded using locale-coding-system (see Locales) if it is non-nil; this can be overridden by binding coding-system-for-write to a coding system of you choice (see Explicit Encoding and Decoding).
01378| 
01379| In batch mode, Emacs will enlarge the value of the gc-cons-percentage variable from the default of ‘0.1’ up to ‘1.0’. Batch jobs that are supposed to run for a long time should adjust the limit back down again, because this means that less garbage collection will be performed by default (and more memory consumed).
01380| 
01381| 
01382| *** Session Management
01383| 
01384| Emacs supports the X Session Management Protocol, which is used to suspend and restart applications. In the X Window System, a program called the session manager is responsible for keeping track of the applications that are running. When the X server shuts down, the session manager asks applications to save their state, and delays the actual shutdown until they respond. An application can also cancel the shutdown.
01385| 
01386| When the session manager restarts a suspended session, it directs these applications to individually reload their saved state. It does this by specifying a special command-line argument that says what saved session to restore. For Emacs, this argument is ‘--smid session’.
01387| 
01388| Variable: emacs-save-session-functions ¶
01389| Emacs supports saving state via a hook called emacs-save-session-functions. Emacs runs this hook when the session manager tells it that the window system is shutting down. The functions are called with no arguments, and with the current buffer set to a temporary buffer. Each function can use insert to add Lisp code to this buffer. At the end, Emacs saves the buffer in a file, called the session file.
01390| 
01391| Subsequently, when the session manager restarts Emacs, it loads the session file automatically (see Loading). This is performed by a function named emacs-session-restore, which is called during startup. See Summary: Sequence of Actions at Startup.
01392| 
01393| If a function in emacs-save-session-functions returns non-nil, Emacs tells the session manager to cancel the shutdown.
01394| 
01395| Here is an example that just inserts some text into *scratch* when Emacs is restarted by the session manager.
01396| 
01397| (add-hook 'emacs-save-session-functions 'save-yourself-test)
01398| 
01399| (defun save-yourself-test ()
01400|   (insert
01401|    (format "%S" '(with-current-buffer "*scratch*"
01402|                    (insert "I am restored"))))
01403|   nil)
01404| 
01405| *** Desktop Notifications
01406| 
01407| Emacs is able to send notifications on systems that support the freedesktop.org Desktop Notifications Specification and on MS-Windows. In order to use this functionality on POSIX hosts, Emacs must have been compiled with D-Bus support, and the notifications library must be loaded. See D-Bus in D-Bus integration in Emacs. The following function is supported when D-Bus support is available:
01408| 
01409| Function: notifications-notify &rest params ¶
01410| This function sends a notification to the desktop via D-Bus, consisting of the parameters specified by the params arguments. These arguments should consist of alternating keyword and value pairs. The supported keywords and values are as follows:
01411| 
01412| :bus bus
01413| The D-Bus bus. This argument is needed only if a bus other than :session shall be used.
01414| 
01415| :title title
01416| The notification title.
01417| 
01418| :body text
01419| The notification body text. Depending on the implementation of the notification server, the text could contain HTML markups, like ‘"<b>bold text</b>"’, hyperlinks, or images. Special HTML characters must be encoded, as ‘"Contact &lt;postmaster@localhost&gt;!"’.
01420| 
01421| :app-name name
01422| The name of the application sending the notification. The default is notifications-application-name.
01423| 
01424| :replaces-id id
01425| The notification id that this notification replaces. id must be the result of a previous notifications-notify call.
01426| 
01427| :app-icon icon-file
01428| The file name of the notification icon. If set to nil, no icon is displayed. The default is notifications-application-icon.
01429| 
01430| :actions (key title key title ...)
01431| A list of actions to be applied. key and title are both strings. The default action (usually invoked by clicking the notification) should have a key named ‘"default"’. The title can be anything, though implementations are free not to display it.
01432| 
01433| :timeout timeout
01434| The timeout time in milliseconds since the display of the notification at which the notification should automatically close. If −1, the notification’s expiration time is dependent on the notification server’s settings, and may vary for the type of notification. If 0, the notification never expires. Default value is −1.
01435| 
01436| :urgency urgency
01437| The urgency level. It can be low, normal, or critical.
01438| 
01439| :action-items
01440| When this keyword is given, the title string of the actions is interpreted as icon name.
01441| 
01442| :category category
01443| The type of notification this is, a string. See the Desktop Notifications Specification for a list of standard categories.
01444| 
01445| :desktop-entry filename
01446| This specifies the name of the desktop filename representing the calling program, like ‘"emacs"’.
01447| 
01448| :image-data (width height rowstride has-alpha bits channels data)
01449| This is a raw data image format that describes the width, height, rowstride, whether there is an alpha channel, bits per sample, channels and image data, respectively.
01450| 
01451| :image-path path
01452| This is represented either as a URI (‘file://’ is the only URI schema supported right now) or a name in a freedesktop.org-compliant icon theme from ‘$XDG_DATA_DIRS/icons’.
01453| 
01454| :sound-file filename
01455| The path to a sound file to play when the notification pops up.
01456| 
01457| :sound-name name
01458| A themable named sound from the freedesktop.org sound naming specification from ‘$XDG_DATA_DIRS/sounds’, to play when the notification pops up. Similar to the icon name, only for sounds. An example would be ‘"message-new-instant"’.
01459| 
01460| :suppress-sound
01461| Causes the server to suppress playing any sounds, if it has that ability.
01462| 
01463| :resident
01464| When set the server will not automatically remove the notification when an action has been invoked. The notification will remain resident in the server until it is explicitly removed by the user or by the sender. This hint is likely only useful when the server has the :persistence capability.
01465| 
01466| :transient
01467| When set the server will treat the notification as transient and by-pass the server’s persistence capability, if it should exist.
01468| 
01469| :x position
01470| :y position
01471| Specifies the X, Y location on the screen that the notification should point to. Both arguments must be used together.
01472| 
01473| :on-action function
01474| Function to call when an action is invoked. The notification id and the key of the action are passed as arguments to the function.
01475| 
01476| :on-close function
01477| Function to call when the notification has been closed by timeout or by the user. The function receive the notification id and the closing reason as arguments:
01478| 
01479| expired if the notification has expired
01480| dismissed if the notification was dismissed by the user
01481| close-notification if the notification was closed by a call to notifications-close-notification
01482| undefined if the notification server hasn’t provided a reason
01483| Which parameters are accepted by the notification server can be checked via notifications-get-capabilities.
01484| 
01485| This function returns a notification id, an integer, which can be used to manipulate the notification item with notifications-close-notification or the :replaces-id argument of another notifications-notify call. For example:
01486| 
01487| (defun my-on-action-function (id key)
01488|   (message "Message %d, key \"%s\" pressed" id key))
01489|      ⇒ my-on-action-function
01490| 
01491| (defun my-on-close-function (id reason)
01492|   (message "Message %d, closed due to \"%s\"" id reason))
01493|      ⇒ my-on-close-function
01494| 
01495| (notifications-notify
01496|  :title "Title"
01497|  :body "This is <b>important</b>."
01498|  :actions '("Confirm" "I agree" "Refuse" "I disagree")
01499|  :on-action 'my-on-action-function
01500|  :on-close 'my-on-close-function)
01501|      ⇒ 22
01502| 
01503| A message window opens on the desktop.  Press ``I agree''.
01504|      ⇒ Message 22, key "Confirm" pressed
01505|         Message 22, closed due to "dismissed"
01506| Function: notifications-close-notification id &optional bus ¶
01507| This function closes a notification with identifier id. bus can be a string denoting a D-Bus connection, the default is :session.
01508| 
01509| Function: notifications-get-capabilities &optional bus ¶
01510| Returns the capabilities of the notification server, a list of symbols. bus can be a string denoting a D-Bus connection, the default is :session. The following capabilities can be expected:
01511| 
01512| :actions
01513| The server will provide the specified actions to the user.
01514| 
01515| :body
01516| Supports body text.
01517| 
01518| :body-hyperlinks
01519| The server supports hyperlinks in the notifications.
01520| 
01521| :body-images
01522| The server supports images in the notifications.
01523| 
01524| :body-markup
01525| Supports markup in the body text.
01526| 
01527| :icon-multi
01528| The server will render an animation of all the frames in a given image array.
01529| 
01530| :icon-static
01531| Supports display of exactly 1 frame of any given image array. This value is mutually exclusive with :icon-multi.
01532| 
01533| :persistence
01534| The server supports persistence of notifications.
01535| 
01536| :sound
01537| The server supports sounds on notifications.
01538| 
01539| Further vendor-specific caps start with :x-vendor, like :x-gnome-foo-cap.
01540| 
01541| Function: notifications-get-server-information &optional bus ¶
01542| Return information on the notification server, a list of strings. bus can be a string denoting a D-Bus connection, the default is :session. The returned list is (name vendor version spec-version).
01543| 
01544| name
01545| The product name of the server.
01546| 
01547| vendor
01548| The vendor name. For example, ‘"KDE"’, ‘"GNOME"’.
01549| 
01550| version
01551| The server’s version number.
01552| 
01553| spec-version
01554| The specification version the server is compliant with.
01555| 
01556| If spec_version is nil, the server supports a specification prior to ‘"1.0"’.
01557| 
01558| When Emacs runs on MS-Windows as a GUI session, it supports a small subset of the D-Bus notifications functionality via a native primitive:
01559| 
01560| Function: w32-notification-notify &rest params ¶
01561| This function displays an MS-Windows tray notification as specified by params. MS-Windows tray notifications are displayed in a balloon from an icon in the notification area of the taskbar.
01562| 
01563| Value is the integer unique ID of the notification that can be used to remove the notification using w32-notification-close, described below. If the function fails, the return value is nil.
01564| 
01565| The arguments params are specified as keyword/value pairs. All the parameters are optional, but if no parameters are specified, the function will do nothing and return nil.
01566| 
01567| The following parameters are supported:
01568| 
01569| :icon icon
01570| Display icon in the system tray. If icon is a string, it should specify a file name from which to load the icon; the specified file should be a .ico Windows icon file. If icon is not a string, or if this parameter is not specified, the standard Emacs icon will be used.
01571| 
01572| :tip tip
01573| Use tip as the tooltip for the notification. If tip is a string, this is the text of a tooltip that will be shown when the mouse pointer hovers over the tray icon added by the notification. If tip is not a string, or if this parameter is not specified, the default tooltip text is ‘Emacs notification’. The tooltip text can be up to 127 characters long (63 on Windows versions before W2K). Longer strings will be truncated.
01574| 
01575| :level level
01576| Notification severity level, one of info, warning, or error. If given, the value determines the icon displayed to the left of the notification title, but only if the :title parameter (see below) is also specified and is a string.
01577| 
01578| :title title
01579| The title of the notification. If title is a string, it is displayed in a larger font immediately above the body text. The title text can be up to 63 characters long; longer text will be truncated.
01580| 
01581| :body body
01582| The body of the notification. If body is a string, it specifies the text of the notification message. Use embedded newlines to control how the text is broken into lines. The body text can be up to 255 characters long, and will be truncated if it’s longer. Unlike with D-Bus, the body text should be plain text, with no markup.
01583| 
01584| Note that versions of Windows before W2K support only :icon and :tip. The other parameters can be passed, but they will be ignored on those old systems.
01585| 
01586| There can be at most one active notification at any given time. An active notification must be removed by calling w32-notification-close before a new one can be shown.
01587| 
01588| To remove the notification and its icon from the taskbar, use the following function:
01589| 
01590| Function: w32-notification-close id ¶
01591| This function removes the tray notification given by its unique id.
01592| 
01593| 
01594| *** Notifications on File Changes
01595| 
01596| Several operating systems support watching of filesystems for changes to files or their attributes. If configured properly, Emacs links a respective library like inotify, kqueue, gfilenotify, or w32notify statically. These libraries enable watching of filesystems on the local machine.
01597| 
01598| It is also possible to watch filesystems on remote machines, see Remote Files in The GNU Emacs Manual. This does not depend on one of the libraries linked to Emacs.
01599| 
01600| Since all these libraries emit different events upon notified file changes, Emacs provides a special library filenotify which presents a unified interface to applications. Lisp programs that want to receive file notifications should always use this library in preference to the native ones. This section documents the filenotify library functions and variables.
01601| 
01602| Function: file-notify-add-watch file flags callback ¶
01603| Add a watch for filesystem events pertaining to file. This arranges for filesystem events pertaining to file to be reported to Emacs.
01604| 
01605| The returned value is a descriptor for the added watch. Its type depends on the underlying library, and in general cannot be assumed to be an integer as in the example below. It should be used for comparison by equal only.
01606| 
01607| If the file cannot be watched for some reason, this function signals a file-notify-error error.
01608| 
01609| Sometimes, mounted filesystems cannot be watched for file changes. This is not detected by this function, and so a non-nil return value does not guarantee that changes on file will be actually notified.
01610| 
01611| If file is a symlink, it doesn’t follow that link. Just file itself will be watched.
01612| 
01613| flags is a list of conditions to set what will be watched for. It can include the following symbols:
01614| 
01615| change
01616| watch for changes in file’s contents
01617| 
01618| attribute-change
01619| watch for changes in file attributes, like permissions or modification time
01620| 
01621| If file is a directory, change watches for file creation and deletion in that directory. Some of the native file notification libraries also report file changes in that case. This does not work recursively.
01622| 
01623| When any event happens, Emacs will call the callback function passing it a single argument event, which is of the form
01624| 
01625| (descriptor action file [file1])
01626| descriptor is the same object as the one returned by this function. action is the description of the event. It could be any one of the following symbols:
01627| 
01628| created
01629| file was created
01630| 
01631| deleted
01632| file was deleted
01633| 
01634| changed
01635| file’s contents has changed; with w32notify library, reports attribute changes as well
01636| 
01637| renamed
01638| file has been renamed to file1
01639| 
01640| attribute-changed
01641| a file attribute was changed
01642| 
01643| stopped
01644| watching file has stopped
01645| 
01646| Note that the w32notify library does not report attribute-changed events. When some file’s attribute, like permissions or modification time, has changed, this library reports a changed event. Likewise, the kqueue library does not reliably report file attribute changes when watching a directory.
01647| 
01648| The stopped event means that watching the file has been discontinued. This could be because file-notify-rm-watch was called (see below), or because the file being watched was deleted, or due to another error reported from the underlying library which makes further watching impossible.
01649| 
01650| file and file1 are the name of the file(s) whose event is being reported. For example:
01651| 
01652| (require 'filenotify)
01653|      ⇒ filenotify
01654| 
01655| (defun my-notify-callback (event)
01656|   (message "Event %S" event))
01657|      ⇒ my-notify-callback
01658| 
01659| (file-notify-add-watch
01660|   "/tmp" '(change attribute-change) 'my-notify-callback)
01661|      ⇒ 35025468
01662| 
01663| (write-region "foo" nil "/tmp/foo")
01664|      ⇒ Event (35025468 created "/tmp/.#foo")
01665|         Event (35025468 created "/tmp/foo")
01666|         Event (35025468 changed "/tmp/foo")
01667|         Event (35025468 deleted "/tmp/.#foo")
01668| 
01669| (write-region "bla" nil "/tmp/foo")
01670|      ⇒ Event (35025468 created "/tmp/.#foo")
01671|         Event (35025468 changed "/tmp/foo")
01672|         Event (35025468 deleted "/tmp/.#foo")
01673| 
01674| (set-file-modes "/tmp/foo" (default-file-modes) 'nofollow)
01675|      ⇒ Event (35025468 attribute-changed "/tmp/foo")
01676| Whether the action renamed is returned depends on the used watch library. Otherwise, the actions deleted and created could be returned in a random order.
01677| 
01678| (rename-file "/tmp/foo" "/tmp/bla")
01679|      ⇒ Event (35025468 renamed "/tmp/foo" "/tmp/bla")
01680| 
01681| (delete-file "/tmp/bla")
01682|      ⇒ Event (35025468 deleted "/tmp/bla")
01683| Function: file-notify-rm-watch descriptor ¶
01684| Removes an existing file watch specified by its descriptor. descriptor should be an object returned by file-notify-add-watch.
01685| 
01686| Command: file-notify-rm-all-watches ¶
01687| Removes all existing file notification watches from Emacs.
01688| 
01689| Use this command with caution, because it could have unexpected side effects on packages relying on file watches. It is intended mainly for debugging purposes, or when Emacs has been stalled.
01690| 
01691| Function: file-notify-valid-p descriptor ¶
01692| Checks a watch specified by its descriptor for validity. descriptor should be an object returned by file-notify-add-watch.
01693| 
01694| A watch can become invalid if the file or directory it watches is deleted, or if the watcher thread exits abnormally for any other reason. Removing the watch by calling file-notify-rm-watch also makes it invalid.
01695| 
01696| (make-directory "/tmp/foo")
01697|      ⇒ Event (35025468 created "/tmp/foo")
01698| 
01699| (setq desc
01700|       (file-notify-add-watch
01701|         "/tmp/foo" '(change) 'my-notify-callback))
01702|      ⇒ 11359632
01703| 
01704| (file-notify-valid-p desc)
01705|      ⇒ t
01706| 
01707| (write-region "bla" nil "/tmp/foo/bla")
01708|      ⇒ Event (11359632 created "/tmp/foo/.#bla")
01709|         Event (11359632 created "/tmp/foo/bla")
01710|         Event (11359632 changed "/tmp/foo/bla")
01711|         Event (11359632 deleted "/tmp/foo/.#bla")
01712| 
01713| ;; Deleting a file in the directory doesn't invalidate the watch.
01714| (delete-file "/tmp/foo/bla")
01715|      ⇒ Event (11359632 deleted "/tmp/foo/bla")
01716| 
01717| (write-region "bla" nil "/tmp/foo/bla")
01718|      ⇒ Event (11359632 created "/tmp/foo/.#bla")
01719|         Event (11359632 created "/tmp/foo/bla")
01720|         Event (11359632 changed "/tmp/foo/bla")
01721|         Event (11359632 deleted "/tmp/foo/.#bla")
01722| 
01723| ;; Deleting the directory invalidates the watch.
01724| ;; Events arrive for different watch descriptors.
01725| (delete-directory "/tmp/foo" 'recursive)
01726|      ⇒ Event (35025468 deleted "/tmp/foo")
01727|         Event (11359632 deleted "/tmp/foo/bla")
01728|         Event (11359632 deleted "/tmp/foo")
01729|         Event (11359632 stopped "/tmp/foo")
01730| 
01731| (file-notify-valid-p desc)
01732|      ⇒ nil
01733| 
01734| *** Dynamically Loaded Libraries
01735| 
01736| A dynamically loaded library is a library that is loaded on demand, when its facilities are first needed. Emacs supports such on-demand loading of support libraries for some of its features.
01737| 
01738| Variable: dynamic-library-alist ¶
01739| This is an alist of dynamic libraries and external library files implementing them.
01740| 
01741| Each element is a list of the form (library files…), where the car is a symbol representing a supported external library, and the rest are strings giving alternate filenames for that library.
01742| 
01743| Emacs tries to load the library from the files in the order they appear in the list; if none is found, the Emacs session won’t have access to that library, and the features it provides will be unavailable.
01744| 
01745| Image support on some platforms uses this facility. Here’s an example of setting this variable for supporting images on MS-Windows:
01746| 
01747| (setq dynamic-library-alist
01748|       '((xpm "libxpm.dll" "xpm4.dll" "libXpm-nox4.dll")
01749|         (png "libpng12d.dll" "libpng12.dll" "libpng.dll"
01750|              "libpng13d.dll" "libpng13.dll")
01751|         (jpeg "jpeg62.dll" "libjpeg.dll" "jpeg-62.dll"
01752|               "jpeg.dll")
01753|         (tiff "libtiff3.dll" "libtiff.dll")
01754|         (gif "giflib4.dll" "libungif4.dll" "libungif.dll")
01755|         (svg "librsvg-2-2.dll")
01756|         (gdk-pixbuf "libgdk_pixbuf-2.0-0.dll")
01757|         (glib "libglib-2.0-0.dll")
01758|         (gobject "libgobject-2.0-0.dll")))
01759| Note that image types pbm and xbm do not need entries in this variable because they do not depend on external libraries and are always available in Emacs.
01760| 
01761| Also note that this variable is not meant to be a generic facility for accessing external libraries; only those already known by Emacs can be loaded through it.
01762| 
01763| This variable is ignored if the given library is statically linked into Emacs.
01764| 
01765| 
01766| *** Security Considerations
01767| 
01768| Like any application, Emacs can be run in a secure environment, where the operating system enforces rules about access and the like. With some care, Emacs-based applications can also be part of a security perimeter that checks such rules. Although the default settings for Emacs work well for a typical software development environment, they may require adjustment in environments containing untrusted users that may include attackers. Here is a compendium of security issues that may be helpful if you are developing such applications. It is by no means complete; it is intended to give you an idea of the security issues involved, rather than to be a security checklist.
01769| 
01770| File local variables ¶
01771| A file that Emacs visits can contain variable settings that affect the buffer visiting that file; See File Local Variables. Similarly, a directory can specify local variable values common to all files in that directory; see Directory Local Variables. Although Emacs takes some effort to protect against misuse of these variables, a security hole can be created merely by a package setting safe-local-variable too optimistically, a problem that is all too common. To disable this feature for both files and directories, set enable-local-variables to nil.
01772| 
01773| Access control
01774| Although Emacs normally respects access permissions of the underlying operating system, in some cases it handles accesses specially. For example, file names can have handlers that treat the files specially, with their own access checking. See Making Certain File Names “Magic”. Also, a buffer can be read-only even if the corresponding file is writable, and vice versa, which can result in messages such as ‘File passwd is write-protected; try to save anyway? (yes or no)’. See Read-Only Buffers.
01775| 
01776| Authentication
01777| Emacs has several functions that deal with passwords, e.g., read-passwd. See Reading a Password. Although these functions do not attempt to broadcast passwords to the world, their implementations are not proof against determined attackers with access to Emacs internals. For example, even if Elisp code uses clear-string to scrub a password from its memory after using it, remnants of the password may still reside in the garbage-collected free list. See Modifying Strings.
01778| 
01779| Code injection
01780| Emacs can send commands to many other applications, and applications should take care that strings sent as operands of these commands are not misinterpreted as directives. For example, when using a shell command to rename a file a to b, do not simply use the string mv a b, because either file name might start with ‘-’, or might contain shell metacharacters like ‘;’. Although functions like shell-quote-argument can help avoid this sort of problem, they are not panaceas; for example, on a POSIX platform shell-quote-argument quotes shell metacharacters but not leading ‘-’. On MS-Windows, quoting for ‘%’ assumes none of the environment variables have ‘^’ in their name. See Shell Arguments. Typically it is safer to use call-process than a subshell. See Creating a Synchronous Process. And it is safer yet to use builtin Emacs functions; for example, use (rename-file "a" "b" t) instead of invoking mv. See Changing File Names and Attributes.
01781| 
01782| Coding systems
01783| Emacs attempts to infer the coding systems of the files and network connections it accesses. See Coding Systems. If Emacs infers incorrectly, or if the other parties to the network connection disagree with Emacs’s inferences, the resulting system could be unreliable. Also, even when it infers correctly, Emacs often can use bytes that other programs cannot. For example, although to Emacs the null byte is just a character like any other, many other applications treat it as a string terminator and mishandle strings or files containing null bytes.
01784| 
01785| Environment and configuration variables
01786| POSIX specifies several environment variables that can affect how Emacs behaves. Any environment variable whose name consists entirely of uppercase ASCII letters, digits, and the underscore may affect the internal behavior of Emacs. Emacs uses several such variables, e.g., EMACSLOADPATH. See Library Search. On some platforms some environment variables (e.g., PATH, POSIXLY_CORRECT, SHELL, TMPDIR) need to have properly-configured values in order to get standard behavior for any utility Emacs might invoke. Even seemingly-benign variables like TZ may have security implications. See Operating System Environment.
01787| 
01788| Emacs has customization and other variables with similar considerations. For example, if the variable shell-file-name specifies a shell with nonstandard behavior, an Emacs-based application may misbehave.
01789| 
01790| Installation
01791| When Emacs is installed, if the installation directory hierarchy can be modified by untrusted users, the application cannot be trusted. This applies also to the directory hierarchies of the programs that Emacs uses, and of the files that Emacs reads and writes.
01792| 
01793| Network access
01794| Emacs often accesses the network, and you may want to configure it to avoid network accesses that it would normally do. For example, unless you set tramp-mode to nil, file names using a certain syntax are interpreted as being network files, and are retrieved across the network. See The Tramp Manual in The Tramp Manual.
01795| 
01796| Race conditions
01797| Emacs applications have the same sort of race-condition issues that other applications do. For example, even when (file-readable-p "foo.txt") returns t, it could be that foo.txt is unreadable because some other program changed the file’s permissions between the call to file-readable-p and now. See Testing Accessibility.
01798| 
01799| Resource limits
01800| When Emacs exhausts memory or other operating system resources, its behavior can be less reliable, in that computations that ordinarily run to completion may abort back to the top level. This may cause Emacs to neglect operations that it normally would have done.
01801| 
01802| 
01803| 
01804| * Cutomization
01805| Users of Emacs can customize variables and faces without writing Lisp code, by using the Customize interface. See Easy Customization in The GNU Emacs Manual. This chapter describes how to define customization items that users can interact with through the Customize interface.
01806| 
01807| Customization items include customizable variables, which are defined with the defcustom macro; customizable faces, which are defined with defface (described separately in Defining Faces); and customization groups, defined with defgroup, which act as containers for groups of related customization items.
01808| 
01809| *** Common Item Keywords
01810| 
01811| The customization declarations that we will describe in the next few sections—defcustom, defgroup, etc.—all accept keyword arguments (see Variables that Never Change) for specifying various information. This section describes keywords that apply to all types of customization declarations.
01812| 
01813| All of these keywords, except :tag, can be used more than once in a given item. Each use of the keyword has an independent effect. The keyword :tag is an exception because any given item can only display one name.
01814| 
01815| :tag label ¶
01816| Use label, a string, instead of the item’s name, to label the item in customization menus and buffers. Don’t use a tag which is substantially different from the item’s real name; that would cause confusion.
01817| 
01818| :group group
01819| Put this customization item in group group. If this keyword is missing from a customization item, it’ll be placed in the same group that was last defined (in the current file).
01820| 
01821| When you use :group in a defgroup, it makes the new group a subgroup of group.
01822| 
01823| If you use this keyword more than once, you can put a single item into more than one group. Displaying any of those groups will show this item. Please don’t overdo this, since the result would be annoying.
01824| 
01825| :link link-data ¶
01826| Include an external link after the documentation string for this item. This is a sentence containing a button that references some other documentation.
01827| 
01828| There are several alternatives you can use for link-data:
01829| 
01830| (custom-manual info-node)
01831| Link to an Info node; info-node is a string which specifies the node name, as in "(emacs)Top". The link appears as ‘[Manual]’ in the customization buffer and enters the built-in Info reader on info-node.
01832| 
01833| (info-link info-node)
01834| Like custom-manual except that the link appears in the customization buffer with the Info node name.
01835| 
01836| (url-link url)
01837| Link to a web page; url is a string which specifies the URL. The link appears in the customization buffer as url and invokes the WWW browser specified by browse-url-browser-function.
01838| 
01839| (emacs-commentary-link library)
01840| Link to the commentary section of a library; library is a string which specifies the library name. See Conventional Headers for Emacs Libraries.
01841| 
01842| (emacs-library-link library)
01843| Link to an Emacs Lisp library file; library is a string which specifies the library name.
01844| 
01845| (file-link file)
01846| Link to a file; file is a string which specifies the name of the file to visit with find-file when the user invokes this link.
01847| 
01848| (function-link function)
01849| Link to the documentation of a function; function is a string which specifies the name of the function to describe with describe-function when the user invokes this link.
01850| 
01851| (variable-link variable)
01852| Link to the documentation of a variable; variable is a string which specifies the name of the variable to describe with describe-variable when the user invokes this link.
01853| 
01854| (face-link face)
01855| Link to the documentation of a face; face is a string which specifies the name of the face to describe with describe-face when the user invokes this link.
01856| 
01857| (custom-group-link group)
01858| Link to another customization group. Invoking it creates a new customization buffer for group.
01859| 
01860| You can specify the text to use in the customization buffer by adding :tag name after the first element of the link-data; for example, (info-link :tag "foo" "(emacs)Top") makes a link to the Emacs manual which appears in the buffer as ‘foo’.
01861| 
01862| You can use this keyword more than once, to add multiple links.
01863| 
01864| :load file ¶
01865| Load file file (a string) before displaying this customization item (see Loading). Loading is done with load, and only if the file is not already loaded.
01866| 
01867| :require feature ¶
01868| Execute (require 'feature) when your saved customizations set the value of this item. feature should be a symbol.
01869| 
01870| The most common reason to use :require is when a variable enables a feature such as a minor mode, and just setting the variable won’t have any effect unless the code which implements the mode is loaded.
01871| 
01872| :version version ¶
01873| This keyword specifies that the item was first introduced in Emacs version version, or that its default value was changed in that version. The value version must be a string.
01874| 
01875| :package-version '(package . version) ¶
01876| This keyword specifies that the item was first introduced in package version version, or that its meaning or default value was changed in that version. This keyword takes priority over :version.
01877| 
01878| package should be the official name of the package, as a symbol (e.g., MH-E). version should be a string. If the package package is released as part of Emacs, package and version should appear in the value of customize-package-emacs-version-alist.
01879| 
01880| Packages distributed as part of Emacs that use the :package-version keyword must also update the customize-package-emacs-version-alist variable.
01881| 
01882| Variable: customize-package-emacs-version-alist ¶
01883| This alist provides a mapping for the versions of Emacs that are associated with versions of a package listed in the :package-version keyword. Its elements are:
01884| 
01885| (package (pversion . eversion)…)
01886| For each package, which is a symbol, there are one or more elements that contain a package version pversion with an associated Emacs version eversion. These versions are strings. For example, the MH-E package updates this alist with the following:
01887| 
01888| (add-to-list 'customize-package-emacs-version-alist
01889|              '(MH-E ("6.0" . "22.1") ("6.1" . "22.1") ("7.0" . "22.1")
01890|                     ("7.1" . "22.1") ("7.2" . "22.1") ("7.3" . "22.1")
01891|                     ("7.4" . "22.1") ("8.0" . "22.1")))
01892| The value of package needs to be unique and it needs to match the package value appearing in the :package-version keyword. Since the user might see the value in an error message, a good choice is the official name of the package, such as MH-E or Gnus.
01893| 
01894| 
01895| *** Defining Customization Groups
01896| 
01897| Each Emacs Lisp package should have one main customization group which contains all the options, faces and other groups in the package. If the package has a small number of options and faces, use just one group and put everything in it. When there are more than twenty or so options and faces, then you should structure them into subgroups, and put the subgroups under the package’s main customization group. It is OK to put some of the options and faces in the package’s main group alongside the subgroups.
01898| 
01899| The package’s main or only group should be a member of one or more of the standard customization groups. (To display the full list of them, use M-x customize.) Choose one or more of them (but not too many), and add your group to each of them using the :group keyword.
01900| 
01901| The way to declare new customization groups is with defgroup.
01902| 
01903| Macro: defgroup group members doc [keyword value]… ¶
01904| Declare group as a customization group containing members. Do not quote the symbol group. The argument doc specifies the documentation string for the group.
01905| 
01906| The argument members is a list specifying an initial set of customization items to be members of the group. However, most often members is nil, and you specify the group’s members by using the :group keyword when defining those members.
01907| 
01908| If you want to specify group members through members, each element should have the form (name widget). Here name is a symbol, and widget is a widget type for editing that symbol. Useful widgets are custom-variable for a variable, custom-face for a face, and custom-group for a group.
01909| 
01910| When you introduce a new group into Emacs, use the :version keyword in the defgroup; then you need not use it for the individual members of the group.
01911| 
01912| In addition to the common keywords (see Common Item Keywords), you can also use this keyword in defgroup:
01913| 
01914| :prefix prefix ¶
01915| If the name of an item in the group starts with prefix, and the customizable variable custom-unlispify-remove-prefixes is non-nil, the item’s tag will omit prefix. A group can have any number of prefixes.
01916| 
01917| The variables, faces, and subgroups of a group are stored in the custom-group property of the group’s symbol. See Accessing Symbol Properties. The value of that property is a list of pairs whose car is the symbol of the variable or the face or the subgroup, and the cdr is one of the corresponding symbols custom-variable, custom-face, or custom-group.
01918| 
01919| User Option: custom-unlispify-remove-prefixes ¶
01920| If this variable is non-nil, the prefixes specified by a group’s :prefix keyword are omitted from tag names, whenever the user customizes the group.
01921| 
01922| The default value is nil, i.e., the prefix-discarding feature is disabled. This is because discarding prefixes often leads to confusing names for options and faces.
01923| 
01924| 
01925| *** Defining Customization Variables
01926| 
01927| Customizable variables, also called user options, are global Lisp variables whose values can be set through the Customize interface. Unlike other global variables, which are defined with defvar (see Defining Global Variables), customizable variables are defined using the defcustom macro. In addition to calling defvar as a subroutine, defcustom states how the variable should be displayed in the Customize interface, the values it is allowed to take, etc.
01928| 
01929| Macro: defcustom option standard doc [keyword value]… ¶
01930| This macro declares option as a user option (i.e., a customizable variable). You should not quote option.
01931| 
01932| The argument standard is an expression that specifies the standard value for option. Evaluating the defcustom form evaluates standard, but does not necessarily bind the option to that value. If option already has a default value, it is left unchanged. If the user has already saved a customization for option, the user’s customized value is installed as the default value. Otherwise, the result of evaluating standard is installed as the default value.
01933| 
01934| Like defvar, this macro marks option as a special variable, meaning that it should always be dynamically bound. If option is already lexically bound, that lexical binding remains in effect until the binding construct exits. See Scoping Rules for Variable Bindings.
01935| 
01936| The expression standard can be evaluated at various other times, too—whenever the customization facility needs to know option’s standard value. So be sure to use an expression which is harmless to evaluate at any time.
01937| 
01938| The argument doc specifies the documentation string for the variable.
01939| 
01940| If a defcustom does not specify any :group, the last group defined with defgroup in the same file will be used. This way, most defcustom do not need an explicit :group.
01941| 
01942| When you evaluate a defcustom form with C-M-x in Emacs Lisp mode (eval-defun), a special feature of eval-defun arranges to set the variable unconditionally, without testing whether its value is void. (The same feature applies to defvar, see Defining Global Variables.) Using eval-defun on a defcustom that is already defined calls the :set function (see below), if there is one.
01943| 
01944| If you put a defcustom in a pre-loaded Emacs Lisp file (see Building Emacs), the standard value installed at dump time might be incorrect, e.g., because another variable that it depends on has not been assigned the right value yet. In that case, use custom-reevaluate-setting, described below, to re-evaluate the standard value after Emacs starts up.
01945| 
01946| In addition to the keywords listed in Common Item Keywords, this macro accepts the following keywords:
01947| 
01948| :type type
01949| Use type as the data type for this option. It specifies which values are legitimate, and how to display the value (see Customization Types). Every defcustom should specify a value for this keyword.
01950| 
01951| :options value-list ¶
01952| Specify the list of reasonable values for use in this option. The user is not restricted to using only these values, but they are offered as convenient alternatives.
01953| 
01954| This is meaningful only for certain types, currently including hook, plist and alist. See the definition of the individual types for a description of how to use :options.
01955| 
01956| Re-evaluating a defcustom form with a different :options value does not clear the values added by previous evaluations, or added by calls to custom-add-frequent-value (see below).
01957| 
01958| :set setfunction ¶
01959| Specify setfunction as the way to change the value of this option when using the Customize interface. The function setfunction should take two arguments, a symbol (the option name) and the new value, and should do whatever is necessary to update the value properly for this option (which may not mean simply setting the option as a Lisp variable); preferably, though, it should not modify its value argument destructively. The default for setfunction is set-default-toplevel-value.
01960| 
01961| If defined, setfunction will also be called when evaluating a defcustom form with C-M-x in Emacs Lisp mode and when the option’s value is changed via the setopt macro (see setopt).
01962| 
01963| If you specify this keyword, the variable’s documentation string should describe how to do the same job in hand-written Lisp code, either by invoking setfunction directly or by using setopt.
01964| 
01965| :get getfunction ¶
01966| Specify getfunction as the way to extract the value of this option. The function getfunction should take one argument, a symbol, and should return whatever customize should use as the current value for that symbol (which need not be the symbol’s Lisp value). The default is default-toplevel-value.
01967| 
01968| You have to really understand the workings of Custom to use :get correctly. It is meant for values that are treated in Custom as variables but are not actually stored in Lisp variables. It is almost surely a mistake to specify getfunction for a value that really is stored in a Lisp variable.
01969| 
01970| :initialize function ¶
01971| function should be a function used to initialize the variable when the defcustom is evaluated. It should take two arguments, the option name (a symbol) and the value. Here are some predefined functions meant for use in this way:
01972| 
01973| custom-initialize-set
01974| Use the variable’s :set function to initialize the variable, but do not reinitialize it if it is already non-void.
01975| 
01976| custom-initialize-default
01977| Like custom-initialize-set, but use the function set-default-toplevel-value to set the variable, instead of the variable’s :set function. This is the usual choice for a variable whose :set function enables or disables a minor mode; with this choice, defining the variable will not call the minor mode function, but customizing the variable will do so.
01978| 
01979| custom-initialize-reset
01980| Always use the :set function to initialize the variable. If the variable is already non-void, reset it by calling the :set function using the current value (returned by the :get method). This is the default :initialize function.
01981| 
01982| custom-initialize-changed
01983| Use the :set function to initialize the variable, if it is already set or has been customized; otherwise, just use set-default-toplevel-value.
01984| 
01985| custom-initialize-delay
01986| This function behaves like custom-initialize-set, but it delays the actual initialization to the next Emacs start. This should be used in files that are preloaded (or for autoloaded variables), so that the initialization is done in the run-time context rather than the build-time context. This also has the side-effect that the (delayed) initialization is performed with the :set function. See Building Emacs.
01987| 
01988| :local value ¶
01989| If the value is t, mark option as automatically buffer-local; if the value is permanent, also set options permanent-local property to t. See Creating and Deleting Buffer-Local Bindings.
01990| 
01991| :risky value ¶
01992| Set the variable’s risky-local-variable property to value (see File Local Variables).
01993| 
01994| :safe function ¶
01995| Set the variable’s safe-local-variable property to function (see File Local Variables).
01996| 
01997| :set-after variables ¶
01998| When setting variables according to saved customizations, make sure to set the variables variables before this one; i.e., delay setting this variable until after those others have been handled. Use :set-after if setting this variable won’t work properly unless those other variables already have their intended values.
01999| 
02000| It is useful to specify the :require keyword for an option that turns on a certain feature. This causes Emacs to load the feature, if it is not already loaded, whenever the option is set. See Common Item Keywords. Here is an example:

(File has more lines. Use 'offset' parameter to read beyond line 2000)
</file>
```

```
86065| A versatile color scheme system with two main temporal variants (Day & Night) and three style flavors (Formal, Smooth, Cyberpunk). Each variant can be rendered with either intense contrast or soft contrast depending on the user's preference.
86066| 
86067| ** Variants
86068| - Day
86069| - Night
86070| 
86071| ** Style Options
86072| - Formal :: clear, sharp, high readability, classic contrast
86073| - Smooth :: gentle, low contrast, easy on the eyes
86074| - Cyberpunk :: extreme contrast, neon-like intensity, digital aesthetics
86075| 
86076| ** Day Variant
86077| *** Formal
86078|     - Intense: White background, black text (#FFFFFF / #000000)
86079|     - Soft: Light grey background, dark grey text (#E6E6E6 / #333333)
86080| *** Smooth
86081|     - Intense: Off-white background, dark grey text (#F9F9F9 / #1A1A1A)
86082|     - Soft: Light grey background, medium grey text (#EEEEEE / #555555)
86083| *** Cyberpunk
86084|     - Intense: Bright white background, neon cyan/magenta text (#FFFFFF / #00FFFF or #FF00FF)
86085|     - Soft: Dark grey background, neon blue/pink text (#222222 / #00CCFF or #FF33AA)

86091| *** Smooth
86092|     - Intense: Very dark navy background, soft white text (#0A0A1A / #EDEDED)
86093|     - Soft: Dark charcoal background, light grey text (#2B2B2B / #DDDDDD)

86100| - "Soft" reduces eye strain with gentler tones while maintaining readability.
86101| - Cyberpunk uses high-saturation accent colors on dark backgrounds to create a futuristic, digital aesthetic.

86105| * Org Roam + Gitmal Integration Blueprint
86106|   :PROPERTIES:
86107|   :AUTHOR: Shapeshifter
86108|   :DATE: 2026-01-02
86109|   :END:

86112|    - ~/org-roam/
86113|      - notes/           ; your org-roam nodes
86114|      - assets/          ; images, diagrams, PDFs
86115|      - config.el        ; org-roam configuration
86116|      - org-roam.db      ; generated DB (optional to gitignore)
86117|    - ~/org-roam/.git    ; git repository

86120|    - Ensure each org node has:
86121|      - #+TITLE: Node Title
86122|      - #+DATE: YYYY-MM-DD
86123|      - #+OPTIONS: toc:nil
86124|      - Tags and properties for filtering
86125|    - Export org files to Markdown for Gitmal:
86126|      #+BEGIN_SRC emacs-lisp :tangle no
86127|      (require 'ox-md)
86128|      (org-md-export-to-markdown)
86129|      #+END_SRC
86130|    - Alternatively, export to HTML:
86131|      #+BEGIN_SRC emacs-lisp :tangle no

86133|      (org-html-export-to-html)
86134|      #+END_SRC

86138|    - Initialize repo: `git init`
86139|    - Stage all: `git add .`
86140|    - Commit: `git commit -m "Initial Org Roam export"`

86142|      #+BEGIN_SRC sh :tangle no

86145|      #+END_SRC

86152|      #+END_SRC

86156|      cd /path/to/org-roam
86157|      gitmal build -o site
```
